Learning distributions from samples is a central question in statistics and machine learning.While significant progress has been achieved in property testing and estimation based on independent and identically distributed (iid) data, for many applications, most notably natural language processing, two new challenges arise:(a) Modeling data as independent observations fails to capture their temporal dependency;(b) Distributions are commonly supported on a large domain whose cardinality is comparable to or even exceeds the sample size.Continuing the progress made in [FOPS16, HOP18], in this paper we study the following prediction problem with dependent data modeled as Markov chains.

Suppose X1,X2,…subscript𝑋1subscript𝑋2…X_{1},X_{2},\dots is a stationary first-order Markov chain on state space [k]≜{1,…,k}≜delimited-[]𝑘1…𝑘[k]\triangleq\left\{1,\dots,k\right\} with unknown statistics.Observing a trajectory Xn≜(X1,…,Xn)≜superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛X^{n}\triangleq(X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}), the goal is to predict the next state Xn+1subscript𝑋𝑛1X_{n+1} by estimating its distribution conditioned on the present data.We use the Kullback-Leibler (KL) divergence as the loss function:For distributions P=[p1,…,pk],Q=[q1,…,qk]formulae-sequence𝑃subscript𝑝1…subscript𝑝𝑘𝑄subscript𝑞1…subscript𝑞𝑘P=\left[p_{1},\dots,p_{k}\right],Q=\left[q_{1},\dots,q_{k}\right], D​(P∥Q)=∑i=1kpi​log⁡piqi𝐷conditional𝑃𝑄superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑘subscript𝑝𝑖subscript𝑝𝑖subscript𝑞𝑖D(P\|Q)=\sum_{i=1}^{k}p_{i}\log\frac{p_{i}}{q_{i}} if pi=0subscript𝑝𝑖0p_{i}=0 whenever qi=0subscript𝑞𝑖0q_{i}=0 and D​(P∥Q)=∞𝐷conditional𝑃𝑄D(P\|Q)=\infty otherwise.The minimax prediction risk is given by𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄k,nsubscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑘𝑛\displaystyle\mathsf{Risk}_{k,n}≜infM^supπ,M𝔼[D(M(⋅|Xn)∥M^(⋅|Xn))]=infM^supπ,M∑i=1k𝔼[D(M(⋅|i)∥M^(⋅|i))𝟏{Xn=i}]\displaystyle\triangleq\inf_{\widehat{M}}\sup_{\pi,M}\mathbb{E}[D(M(\cdot|X_{n})\|\widehat{M}(\cdot|X_{n}))]=\inf_{\widehat{M}}\sup_{\pi,M}\sum_{i=1}^{k}\mathbb{E}[D(M(\cdot|i)\|\widehat{M}(\cdot|i)){\mathbf{1}_{\left\{{X_{n}=i}\right\}}}](1)where the supremum is taken over all stationary distributions π𝜋\pi and transition matrices M𝑀M (row-stochastic) such that π​M=π𝜋𝑀𝜋\pi M=\pi,the infimum is taken over all estimators M^=M^​(X1,…,Xn)^𝑀^𝑀subscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛\widehat{M}=\widehat{M}(X_{1},\dots,X_{n}) that are proper Markov kernels (i.e. rows sum to 1), and M(⋅|i)M(\cdot|i) denotes the i𝑖ith row of M𝑀M.Our main objective is to characterize this minimax risk within universal constant factors as a function of n𝑛n and k𝑘k.

The prediction problem (1) is distinct from the parameter estimation problem such as estimating the transition matrix [Bar51, AG57, Bil61, WK19] or its properties [CS00, KV16, HJL+18, HKL+19] in that the quantity to be estimated (conditional distribution of the next state) depends on the sample path itself. This is precisely what renders the prediction problem closely relevant to natural applications such as autocomplete and text generation. In addition, this formulation allows more flexibility with far less assumptions compared to the estimation framework.For example, if certain state has very small probability under the stationary distribution, consistent estimation of the transition matrix with respect to usual loss function, e.g. squared risk, may not be possible, whereas the prediction problem is unencumbered by such rare states.

In the special case of iid data,the prediction problem reduces to estimating the distribution in KL divergence. In this setting the optimal risk is well understood, which is known to be k−1​\over​2​n​(1+o​(1))𝑘1\over2𝑛1𝑜1{k-1\over 2n}(1+o(1))when k𝑘k is fixed and n→∞→𝑛n\to\infty [BFSS02] and Θ​(kn)Θ𝑘𝑛\Theta(\frac{k}{n}) for k=O​(n)𝑘𝑂𝑛k=O(n) [Pan04, KOPS15].111Here and below ≍,≲,≳asymptotically-equalsless-than-or-similar-togreater-than-or-equivalent-to\asymp,\lesssim,\gtrsim or Θ​(⋅),O​(⋅),Ω​(⋅)Θ⋅𝑂⋅Ω⋅\Theta(\cdot),O(\cdot),\Omega(\cdot) denote equality and inequalities up to universal multiplicative constants.Typical in parametric models, this rate kn𝑘𝑛\frac{k}{n} is commonly referred to the “parametric rate”, which leads to a sample complexity that scales proportionally to the number of parameters and inverse proportionally to the desired accuracy.

In the setting of Markov chains, however, the prediction problem is much less understood especially for large state space. Recently the seminal work [FOPS16] showed the surprising result that for stationary Markov chains on two states, the optimal prediction risk satisfies𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄2,n=Θ​(log⁡log⁡n​\over​n),subscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄2𝑛Θ𝑛\over𝑛\displaystyle\mathsf{Risk}_{2,n}=\Theta\left(\log\log n\over n\right),(2)which has a nonparametric rate even when the problem has only two parameters.The follow-up work [HOP18] studied general k𝑘k-state chains and showed a lower bound of Ω​(k​log⁡log⁡nn)Ω𝑘𝑛𝑛\Omega(\frac{k\log\log n}{n})for uniform (not necessarily stationary) initial distribution; however, the upper bound O​(k2​log⁡log⁡nn)𝑂superscript𝑘2𝑛𝑛O(\frac{k^{2}\log\log n}{n}) in [HOP18] relies on implicit assumptions on mixing time such as spectral gap conditions: the proof of the upper bound for prediction (Lemma 7 in the supplement) and for estimation (Lemma 17 of the supplement) is based on Berstein-type concentration results of the empirical transition counts, which depend on spectral gap.The following theorem resolves the optimal risk for k𝑘k-state Markov chains:

Theorem 1 shows that the departure from the parametric rate of k2nsuperscript𝑘2𝑛\frac{k^{2}}{n}, first discovered in [FOPS16, HOP18] for binary chains, is even more pronounced for larger state space.As will become clear in the proof, there is some fundamental difference between two-state and three-state chains, resulting in 𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄3,n=Θ​(log⁡nn)≫𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄2,n=Θ​(log⁡log⁡nn)subscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄3𝑛Θ𝑛𝑛much-greater-thansubscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄2𝑛Θ𝑛𝑛\mathsf{Risk}_{3,n}=\Theta(\frac{\log n}{n})\gg\mathsf{Risk}_{2,n}=\Theta(\frac{\log\log n}{n}).It is instructive to compare the sample complexity for prediction in the iid and Markov model.Denote by d𝑑d the number of parameters, which is k−1𝑘1k-1 for the iid case and k​(k−1)𝑘𝑘1k(k-1) for Markov chains.Define the sample complexity n∗​(d,ϵ)superscript𝑛𝑑italic-ϵn^{*}(d,\epsilon) as the smallest sample size n𝑛n in order to achieve a prescribed prediction risk ϵitalic-ϵ\epsilon.For ϵ=O​(1)italic-ϵ𝑂1\epsilon=O(1), we haven∗​(d,ϵ)≍{dϵiiddϵ​log⁡log⁡1ϵMarkov with 2 statesdϵ​log⁡1ϵMarkov with k≥3 states.asymptotically-equalssuperscript𝑛𝑑italic-ϵcases𝑑italic-ϵiid𝑑italic-ϵ1italic-ϵMarkov with 2 states𝑑italic-ϵ1italic-ϵMarkov with k≥3 statesn^{*}(d,\epsilon)\asymp\begin{cases}\frac{d}{\epsilon}&\text{iid}\\\frac{d}{\epsilon}\log\log\frac{1}{\epsilon}&\text{Markov with $2$ states}\\\frac{d}{\epsilon}\log\frac{1}{\epsilon}&\text{Markov with $k\geq 3$ states}.\end{cases}(7)

At a high level, the nonparametric rates in the Markov model can be attributed to the memory in the data.On the one hand, Theorem 1 as well as (2) affirm that one can obtain meaningful prediction without imposing any mixing conditions;222To see this, it is helpful to consider the extreme case where the chain does not move at all or is periodic, in which case predicting the next state is in fact easy.such decoupling between learning and mixing has also been observed in other problems such as learning linear dynamics [SMT+18, DMM+19].On the other hand, the dependency in the data does lead to a strictly higher sample complexity than that of the iid case; in fact, the lower bound in Theorem 1 is proved by constructing chains with spectral gap as small as O​(1n)𝑂1𝑛O(\frac{1}{n}) (see Section 3).Thus, it is conceivable that with sufficiently favorable mixing conditions, the prediction risk improves over that of the worst case and, at some point, reaches the parametric rate.To make this precise, we focus on Markov chains with a prescribed spectral gap.

It is well-known that for an irreducible and reversible chain, the transition matrix M𝑀M has k𝑘k real eigenvalues satisfying 1=λ1≥λ2≥…​λk≥−11subscript𝜆1subscript𝜆2…subscript𝜆𝑘11=\lambda_{1}\geq\lambda_{2}\geq\dots\lambda_{k}\geq-1.The absolute spectral gap of M𝑀M, defined asγ∗≜1−max⁡{|λi|:i≠1},≜subscript𝛾1:subscript𝜆𝑖𝑖1\gamma_{*}\triangleq 1-\max\left\{\left|\lambda_{i}\right|:i\neq 1\right\},(8)quantifies the memory of the Markov chain. For example, the mixing time is determined by 1/γ∗1superscript𝛾1/\gamma^{*} (relaxation time) up to logarithmic factors.As extreme cases, the chain which does not move (M𝑀M is identity) and which is iid (M𝑀M is rank-one) have spectral gap equal to 0 and 1, respectively.We refer the reader to [LP17] for more background. Note that the definition of absolute spectral gap requires irreducibility and reversibility, thus we restrict ourselves to this class of Markov chains (it is possible to use more general notions such as pseudo spectral gap to quantify the memory of the process, which is beyond the scope of the current paper).Given γ0∈(0,1)subscript𝛾001\gamma_{0}\in(0,1), define ℳk​(γ0)subscriptℳ𝑘subscript𝛾0{\mathcal{M}}_{k}(\gamma_{0}) as the set of transition matrices corresponding to irreducible and reversible chains whose absolute spectral gap exceeds γ0subscript𝛾0\gamma_{0}.Restricting (1) to this subcollection and noticing the stationary distribution here is uniquely determined by M𝑀M, we define the corresponding minimax risk:𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄k,n​(γ0)subscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑘𝑛subscript𝛾0\displaystyle\mathsf{Risk}_{k,n}(\gamma_{0})≜infM^supM∈ℳk​(γ0)𝔼[D(M(⋅|Xn)∥M^(⋅|Xn))]\displaystyle\triangleq\inf_{\widehat{M}}\sup_{M\in{\mathcal{M}}_{k}(\gamma_{0})}\mathbb{E}\left[D(M(\cdot|X_{n})\|\widehat{M}(\cdot|X_{n}))\right](9)

Extending the result (2) of [FOPS16], the following theorem characterizes the optimal prediction risk for two-state chains with prescribed spectral gaps (the case γ0=0subscript𝛾00\gamma_{0}=0 correspond to the minimax rate in [FOPS16] over all binary Markov chains):

Theorem 2 shows that for binary chains, parametric rate O​(1n)𝑂1𝑛O(\frac{1}{n}) is achievable if and only if the spectral gap is nonvanishing. While this holds for bounded state space(see Corollary 4 below), for large state space, it turns out that much weaker conditions on the absolute spectral gap suffice to guarantee the parametric rate O​(k2​\over​n)𝑂superscript𝑘2\over𝑛O({k^{2}\over n}), achieved by the add-one estimator applied to the entire trajectory. In other words, as long as the spectral gap is not excessively small,the prediction risk in the Markov model behaves in the same way as that of an iid model with equal number of parameters. Similar conclusion has been established previously for the sample complexity of estimating the entropy rate of Markov chains in [HJL+18, Theorem 1].

Finally, we address the optimal prediction risk for higher-order Markov chains:

Notably, for binary states, it turns out that the optimal rate Θ​(log⁡log⁡n​\over​n)Θ𝑛\over𝑛\Theta\left(\log\log n\over n\right) for first-order Markov chains determined by [FOPS16] is something very special, as we show that for second-order chains the optimal rate is Θ​(log⁡n​\over​n)Θ𝑛\over𝑛\Theta\left(\log n\over n\right).

The proof of Theorem 1 deviates from existing approaches based on concentration inequalities for Markov chains.For instance, the standard program for analyzing the add-one estimator (5) involvesproving concentration of the empirical counts on their population version, namely, Ni≈n​πisubscript𝑁𝑖𝑛subscript𝜋𝑖N_{i}\approx n\pi_{i} and Ni​j≈n​πi​M​(j|i)subscript𝑁𝑖𝑗𝑛subscript𝜋𝑖𝑀conditional𝑗𝑖N_{ij}\approx n\pi_{i}M(j|i), and bounding the risk in the atypical case by concentration inequalities, such as the Chernoff-type bounds in [Lez98, Pau15], which have been widely usedin recent work on statistical inference with Markov chains [KV16, HJL+18, HOP18, HKL+19, WK19].However, these concentration inequalities inevitably depends on the spectral gap of the Markov chain,leading to results which deteriorate as the spectral gap becomes smaller.For two-state chains, results free of the spectral gap are obtained in [FOPS16] using explicit joint distribution of the transition counts;this refined analysis, however, is difficult to extend to larger state space as the probability mass function of (Ni​j)subscript𝑁𝑖𝑗(N_{ij}) is given by Whittle’s formula [Whi55] which takes an unwieldy determinantal form.

Eschewing concentration-based arguments, the crux of our proof of Theorem 1, for both the upper and lower bound, revolves around the following quantity known as redundancy:𝖱𝖾𝖽k,n≜infQXnsupPXnD​(PXn∥QXn)=infQXnsupPXn∑xnPXn​(xn)​log⁡PXn​(xn)QXn​(xn).≜subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑘𝑛subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptsupremumsubscript𝑃superscript𝑋𝑛𝐷conditionalsubscript𝑃superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptsupremumsubscript𝑃superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptsuperscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝑃superscript𝑋𝑛superscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝑃superscript𝑋𝑛superscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛superscript𝑥𝑛\mathsf{Red}_{k,n}\triangleq\inf_{Q_{X^{n}}}\sup_{P_{X^{n}}}D(P_{X^{n}}\|Q_{X^{n}})=\inf_{Q_{X^{n}}}\sup_{P_{X^{n}}}\sum_{x^{n}}P_{X^{n}}(x^{n})\log\frac{P_{X^{n}}(x^{n})}{Q_{X^{n}}(x^{n})}.(10)Here the supremum is taken over all joint distributions of stationary Markov chains Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n} on k𝑘k states, and the infimum is over all joint distributions QXnsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛Q_{X^{n}}.A central quantity which measures the minimax regret in universal compression, the redundancy (10) corresponds to minimax cumulative risk (namely, the total prediction risk when the sample size ranges from 1 to n𝑛n),while (1) is the individual minimax risk at sample size n𝑛n – see Section 2 for a detailed discussion.We prove the following reduction between prediction risk and redundancy:1n​𝖱𝖾𝖽k−1,n𝗌𝗒𝗆−log⁡kn≲𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄k,n≤1n−1​𝖱𝖾𝖽k,nless-than-or-similar-to1𝑛superscriptsubscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑘1𝑛𝗌𝗒𝗆𝑘𝑛subscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑘𝑛1𝑛1subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑘𝑛\frac{1}{n}\mathsf{Red}_{k-1,n}^{\mathsf{sym}}-\frac{\log k}{n}\lesssim\mathsf{Risk}_{k,n}\leq\frac{1}{n-1}\mathsf{Red}_{k,n}(11)where 𝖱𝖾𝖽𝗌𝗒𝗆superscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝗌𝗒𝗆\mathsf{Red}^{\mathsf{sym}} denotes the redundancy for symmetric Markov chains. The upper bound is standard: thanks to the convexity of the loss function and stationarity of the Markov chain, the risk of the Cesàro-mean estimator (6) can be upper bounded using the cumulative risk and, in turn, the redundancy. The proof of the lower bound is more involved. Given a (k−1)𝑘1(k-1)-state chain, we embed it into a larger state space by introducing a new state, such that with constant probability, the chain starts from and gets stuck at this state for a period time that is approximately uniform in [n]delimited-[]𝑛[n], then enters the original chain. Effectively, this scenario is equivalent to a prediction problem on k−1𝑘1k-1 states with a random (approximately uniform) sample size, whose prediction risk can then be related to the cumulative risk and redundancy.This intuition can be made precise by considering a Bayesian setting, in which the (k−1)𝑘1(k-1)-state chain is randomized according to the least favorable prior for (10), and representing the Bayes risk as conditional mutual information and applying the chain rule.

Given the above reduction in (11), it suffices to show both redundancies therein are on the order of k2n​log⁡nk2superscript𝑘2𝑛𝑛superscript𝑘2\frac{k^{2}}{n}\log\frac{n}{k^{2}}.The redundancy is upper bounded by pointwise redundancy, which replaces the average in (10) by the maximum over all trajectories.Following [DMPW81, CS04], we consider an explicit probability assignment defined by add-one smoothing and using combinatorial arguments to bound the pointwise redundancy, shown optimal by information-theoretic arguments.

The optimal spectral gap-dependent rate in Theorem 2 relies on the key observation in [FOPS16] that, for binary chains, the dominating contribution to the prediction risk comes from trajectories with a single transition, for which we may apply an add-c𝑐c estimator with c𝑐c depending appropriately on the spectral gap. The lower bound is shown using a Bayesian argument similar to that of [HOP18, Theorem 1]. The proof of Theorem 3 relies on more delicate concentration arguments as the spectral gap is allowed to be vanishingly small. Notably, for small k𝑘k, direct application of existing Bernstein inequalities for Markov chains in [Lez98, Pau15] falls short of establishingthe parametric rate of O​(k2n)𝑂superscript𝑘2𝑛O(\frac{k^{2}}{n}) (see Remark 4 in Section 4.2 for details); instead, we use a fourth moment bound which turns out to be well suited for analyzing concentration of empirical counts conditional on the terminal state.

For large k𝑘k, we further improve the spectral gap condition using a simulation argument for Markov chains using independent samples [Bil61, HJL+18]. A key step is a new concentration inequality for D​(P∥P^n,k+1)𝐷conditional𝑃superscriptsubscript^𝑃𝑛𝑘1D(P\|\widehat{P}_{n,k}^{+1}), where P^n,k+1superscriptsubscript^𝑃𝑛𝑘1\widehat{P}_{n,k}^{+1} is the add-one estimator based on n𝑛n iid observations of P𝑃P supported on [k]delimited-[]𝑘[k]:ℙ​(D​(P∥P^n,k+1)≥c⋅kn+𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒𝗅𝗈𝗀​(n)⋅kn)≤1𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒​(n),ℙ𝐷conditional𝑃superscriptsubscript^𝑃𝑛𝑘1⋅𝑐𝑘𝑛⋅𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒𝗅𝗈𝗀𝑛𝑘𝑛1𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒𝑛\displaystyle\mathbb{P}\left(D(P\|\widehat{P}_{n,k}^{+1})\geq c\cdot\frac{k}{n}+\frac{\mathsf{polylog}(n)\cdot\sqrt{k}}{n}\right)\leq\frac{1}{\mathsf{poly}(n)},(12)for some absolute constant c>0𝑐0c>0. Note that an application of the classical concentration inequality of McDiarmid would result in the second term being 𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒𝗅𝗈𝗀​(n)/n𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒𝗅𝗈𝗀𝑛𝑛\mathsf{polylog}(n)/\sqrt{n}, and (12) crucially improves this to 𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒𝗅𝗈𝗀​(n)⋅k/n⋅𝗉𝗈𝗅𝗒𝗅𝗈𝗀𝑛𝑘𝑛\mathsf{polylog}(n)\cdot\sqrt{k}/n.Such an improvement has been recently observed by [MJT+20, Agr20, GR20] in studying the similar quantity D​(P^n∥P)𝐷conditionalsubscript^𝑃𝑛𝑃D(\widehat{P}_{n}\|P) for the (unsmoothed) empirical distribution P^nsubscript^𝑃𝑛\widehat{P}_{n}; however, these results, based on either the method of types or an explicit upper bound of the moment generating function, are not directly applicable to (12) in which the true distribution P𝑃P appears as the first argument in the KL divergence.

The nonasymptotic analysis of the prediction rate for higher-order chains with large alphabets is based on a similar redundancy-based reduction as the first-order chain. However, optimal nonasymptotic redundancy bounds for higher-order chains is more challenging.Notably, in lower bounding redundancy, we need to bound the mutual information from below by upper bounding the squared error of certain estimators.As noted in [TJW18], existing analysis in [Dav83, Sec III] based on simple mixing conditions from [Par62] leads to suboptimal results on large alphabets.To bypass this issue, we show the pseudo spectral gap [Pau15] of the transition matrix of the first-order chain {(Xt+1,…,Xt+m−1)}t=0n−m+1superscriptsubscriptsubscript𝑋𝑡1…subscript𝑋𝑡𝑚1𝑡0𝑛𝑚1\{(X_{t+1},\dots,X_{t+m-1})\}_{t=0}^{n-m+1} is at least a constant. This is accomplished by a careful construction of a prior on mthsuperscript𝑚thm^{\rm th}-order transition matrices with Θ​(km+1)Θsuperscript𝑘𝑚1\Theta\left(k^{m+1}\right) degrees of freedom.

While the exact prediction problem studied in this paper has recently been in focus since [FOPS16, HOP18], there exists a large body of literature on relate works. As mentioned before some of our proof strategies draws inspiration and results from the study of redundancy in universal compression, its connection to mutual information,as well as the perspective of sequential probability assignment as prediction, dating back to [Dav73, DMPW81, Ris84, Sht87, Rya88]. Asymptotic characterization of the minimax redundancy for Markov sources, both average and pointwise, were obtained in [Dav83, Att99, JS02], in the regime of fixed alphabet size k𝑘k and large sample size n𝑛n. Non-asymptotic characterization was obtained in [Dav83] for n≫k2​log⁡kmuch-greater-than𝑛superscript𝑘2𝑘n\gg k^{2}\log k and recently extended to n≍k2asymptotically-equals𝑛superscript𝑘2n\asymp k^{2} in [TJW18], which further showed that the behavior of the redundancy remains unchanged even if the Markov chain is very close to being iid in terms of spectral gap γ∗=1−o​(1)superscript𝛾1𝑜1\gamma^{*}=1-o(1).

The current paper adds to a growing body of literature devoted to statistical learning with dependent data, in particular those dealing with Markov chains.Estimation of the transition matrix [Bar51, AG57, Bil61, Sin64] and testing the order of Markov chains [CS00] have been well studied in the large-sample regime.More recently attention has been shifted towards large state space and nonasymptotics. For example,[WK19] studied the estimation of transition matrix in ℓ∞→ℓ∞→subscriptℓsubscriptℓ\ell_{\infty}\to\ell_{\infty} induced norm for Markov chains with prescribed pseudo spectral gap and minimum probability mass of the stationary distribution, and determined sample complexity bounds up to logarithmic factors. Similar results have been obtained for estimating properties of Markov chains, includingmixing time and spectral gap [HKL+19], entropy rate [KV16, HJL+18, OS20], graph statistics based on random walk [BHOP18], as well as identity testing [DDG18, CB19, WK20, FW21].Most of these results rely on assumptions on the Markov chains such as lower bounds on the spectral gap and the stationary distribution, which afford concentration for sample statistics of Markov chains. In contrast, one of the main contributions in this paper, in particular Theorem 1, is that optimal prediction can be achieved without these assumptions, thereby providing a novel way of tackling these seemingly unavoidable issues. This is ultimately accomplished by information-theoretic and combinatorial techniques from universal compression.

For n∈ℕ𝑛ℕn\in\mathbb{N}, let [n]≜{1,…,n}≜delimited-[]𝑛1…𝑛[n]\triangleq\{1,\ldots,n\}. Denote xn=(x1,…,xn)superscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝑥1…subscript𝑥𝑛x^{n}=(x_{1},\ldots,x_{n}) and xtn=(xt,…,xn)superscriptsubscript𝑥𝑡𝑛subscript𝑥𝑡…subscript𝑥𝑛x_{t}^{n}=(x_{t},\ldots,x_{n}).The distribution of a random variable X𝑋X is denoted by PXsubscript𝑃𝑋P_{X}. In a Bayesian setting, the distribution of a parameter θ𝜃\theta is referred to as a prior, denoted by Pθsubscript𝑃𝜃P_{\theta}.We recall the following definitions from information theory [CK82, CT06].The conditional KL divergence is defined as as an average of KL divergence between conditional distributions:D​(PA|B​‖QA|B|​PB)≜𝔼B∼PB​[D​(PA|B∥QA|B)]=∫PB​(d​b)​D​(PA|B=b∥QA|B=b).≜𝐷subscript𝑃conditional𝐴𝐵delimited-‖|subscript𝑄conditional𝐴𝐵subscript𝑃𝐵subscript𝔼similar-to𝐵subscript𝑃𝐵delimited-[]𝐷conditionalsubscript𝑃conditional𝐴𝐵subscript𝑄conditional𝐴𝐵subscript𝑃𝐵𝑑𝑏𝐷conditionalsubscript𝑃conditional𝐴𝐵𝑏subscript𝑄conditional𝐴𝐵𝑏D(P_{A|B}\|Q_{A|B}|P_{B})\triangleq\mathbb{E}_{B\sim P_{B}}[D(P_{A|B}\|Q_{A|B})]=\int P_{B}(db)D(P_{A|B=b}\|Q_{A|B=b}).(13)The mutual information between random variables A𝐴A and B𝐵B with joint distribution PA​Bsubscript𝑃𝐴𝐵P_{AB} isI​(A;B)≜D​(PB|A​‖PB|​PA)≜𝐼𝐴𝐵𝐷subscript𝑃conditional𝐵𝐴delimited-‖|subscript𝑃𝐵subscript𝑃𝐴I(A;B)\triangleq D(P_{B|A}\|P_{B}|P_{A});similarly, the conditional mutual information is defined asI​(A;B|C)≜D​(PB|A,C​‖PB|C|​PA,C).≜𝐼𝐴conditional𝐵𝐶𝐷subscript𝑃conditional𝐵𝐴𝐶delimited-‖|subscript𝑃conditional𝐵𝐶subscript𝑃𝐴𝐶I(A;B|C)\triangleq D(P_{B|A,C}\|P_{B|C}|P_{A,C}).The following variational representation of (conditional) mutual information is well-knownI​(A;B)=minQB⁡D​(PB|A​‖QB|​PA),I​(A;B|C)=minQB|C⁡D​(PB|A,C​‖QB|C|​PA​C).formulae-sequence𝐼𝐴𝐵subscriptsubscript𝑄𝐵𝐷subscript𝑃conditional𝐵𝐴delimited-‖|subscript𝑄𝐵subscript𝑃𝐴𝐼𝐴conditional𝐵𝐶subscriptsubscript𝑄conditional𝐵𝐶𝐷subscript𝑃conditional𝐵𝐴𝐶delimited-‖|subscript𝑄conditional𝐵𝐶subscript𝑃𝐴𝐶I(A;B)=\min_{Q_{B}}D(P_{B|A}\|Q_{B}|P_{A}),\quad I(A;B|C)=\min_{Q_{B|C}}D(P_{B|A,C}\|Q_{B|C}|P_{AC}).(14)The entropy of a discrete random variables X𝑋X is H​(X)≜∑xPX​(x)​log⁡1PX​(x)≜𝐻𝑋subscript𝑥subscript𝑃𝑋𝑥1subscript𝑃𝑋𝑥H(X)\triangleq\sum_{x}P_{X}(x)\log\frac{1}{P_{X}(x)}.

The rest of the paper is organized as follows.In Section 2 we describe the general paradigm of minimax redundancy and prediction risk and their dual representation in terms of mutual information.We give a general redundancy-based bound on the prediction risk, which, combined with redundancy bounds for Markov chains, leads to the upper bound in Theorem 1.Section 3 presents the lower bound construction, starting from three states and then extending to k𝑘k states.Spectral-gap dependent risk bounds in Theorems 2 and 3 are given in Section 4. Section 5 presents the results and proofs for mthsuperscript𝑚thm^{\text{th}}-order Markov chains.Section 6 discusses the assumptions and implications of our results and related open problems.

For n∈ℕ𝑛ℕn\in\mathbb{N}, let 𝒫={PXn+1|θ:θ∈Θ}𝒫conditional-setsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛1𝜃𝜃Θ{\mathcal{P}}=\{P_{X^{n+1}|\theta}:\theta\in\Theta\} be a collection of joint distributions parameterized by θ𝜃\theta.

Consider a sample Xn≜(X1,…,Xn)≜superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛X^{n}\triangleq(X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}) of size n𝑛n drawn from PXn|θsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃P_{X^{n}|\theta} for some unknown θ∈Θ𝜃Θ\theta\in\Theta. The redundancy of a probability assignment (joint distribution) QXnsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛Q_{X^{n}} is defined as the worst-case KL risk of fitting the joint distribution of Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n}, namely𝖱𝖾𝖽​(QXn)≜supθ∈ΘD​(PXn|θ∥QXn).≜𝖱𝖾𝖽subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptsupremum𝜃Θ𝐷conditionalsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛\mathsf{Red}(Q_{X^{n}})\triangleq\sup_{\theta\in\Theta}D(P_{X^{n}|\theta}\|Q_{X^{n}}).(15)Optimizing over QXnsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛Q_{X^{n}}, the minimax redundancy is defined as𝖱𝖾𝖽n≜infQXn𝖱𝖾𝖽n​(QXn),≜subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑛subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑛subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛\mathsf{Red}_{n}\triangleq\inf_{Q_{X^{n}}}\mathsf{Red}_{n}(Q_{X^{n}}),(16)where the infimum is over all joint distribution QXnsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛Q_{X^{n}}.This quantity can be operationalized as the redundancy (i.e. regret) in the setting of universal data compression, that is, the excess number of bits compared to the optimal compressor of Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n} that knows θ𝜃\theta [CT06, Chapter 13].

The capacity-redundancy theorem (see [Kem74] for a very general result) provides the following mutual information characterization of (16):𝖱𝖾𝖽n=supPθI​(θ;Xn),subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑛subscriptsupremumsubscript𝑃𝜃𝐼𝜃superscript𝑋𝑛\mathsf{Red}_{n}=\sup_{P_{\theta}}I(\theta;X^{n}),(17)where the supremum is over all distributions (priors) Pθsubscript𝑃𝜃P_{\theta} on ΘΘ\Theta.In view of the variational representation (14), this result can be interpreted as a minimax theorem:𝖱𝖾𝖽n=infQXnsupPθD​(PXn|θ​‖QXn|​Pθ)=supPθinfQXnD​(PXn|θ​‖QXn|​Pθ).subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑛subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptsupremumsubscript𝑃𝜃𝐷subscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃delimited-‖|subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑃𝜃subscriptsupremumsubscript𝑃𝜃subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛𝐷subscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃delimited-‖|subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑃𝜃\mathsf{Red}_{n}=\inf_{Q_{X^{n}}}\sup_{P_{\theta}}D(P_{X^{n}|\theta}\|Q_{X^{n}}|P_{\theta})=\sup_{P_{\theta}}\inf_{Q_{X^{n}}}D(P_{X^{n}|\theta}\|Q_{X^{n}}|P_{\theta}).

Typically, for fixed model size and n→∞→𝑛n\to\infty, one expects that 𝖱𝖾𝖽n=d2logn(1+o(1)\mathsf{Red}_{n}=\frac{d}{2}\log n(1+o(1), whered𝑑d is the number of parameters; see [Ris84] for a general theory of this type. Indeed, on a fixed alphabet of size k𝑘k,we have 𝖱𝖾𝖽n=k−12​log⁡n​(1+o​(1))subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑛𝑘12𝑛1𝑜1\mathsf{Red}_{n}=\frac{k-1}{2}\log n(1+o(1)) for iid model [Dav73] and𝖱𝖾𝖽n=km​(k−1)2​log⁡n​(1+o​(1))subscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑛superscript𝑘𝑚𝑘12𝑛1𝑜1\mathsf{Red}_{n}=\frac{k^{m}(k-1)}{2}\log n(1+o(1)) for m𝑚m-order Markov models [Tro74],with more refined asymptotics shown in [XB97, SW12].For large alphabets, nonasymptotic results have also been obtained.For example, for first-order Markov model, 𝖱𝖾𝖽n≍k2​log⁡nk2asymptotically-equalssubscript𝖱𝖾𝖽𝑛superscript𝑘2𝑛superscript𝑘2\mathsf{Red}_{n}\asymp k^{2}\log\frac{n}{k^{2}} provided that n≳k2greater-than-or-equivalent-to𝑛superscript𝑘2n\gtrsim k^{2} [TJW18].

Consider the problem of predicting the next unseen data point Xn+1subscript𝑋𝑛1X_{n+1} based on the observations X1,…,Xnsubscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}, where (X1,…,Xn+1)subscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛1(X_{1},\ldots,X_{n+1}) are jointly distributed as PXn+1|θsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛1𝜃P_{X^{n+1}|\theta} for some unknown θ∈Θ𝜃Θ\theta\in\Theta. Here, an estimator is a distribution (for Xn+1subscript𝑋𝑛1X_{n+1}) as a function of Xnsuperscript𝑋𝑛X^{n}, which, in turn, can be written as a conditional distribution QXn+1|Xnsubscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}}.As such, its worst-case average risk is𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄​(QXn+1|Xn)≜supθ∈ΘD​(PXn+1|Xn,θ​‖QXn+1|Xn|​PXn|θ),≜𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄subscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptsupremum𝜃Θ𝐷subscript𝑃conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛𝜃delimited-‖|subscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃\mathsf{Risk}(Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}})\triangleq\sup_{\theta\in\Theta}D(P_{X_{n+1}|X^{n},\theta}\|Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}}|P_{X^{n}|\theta}),(18)where the conditional KL divergence is defined in (13).The minimax prediction risk is then defined as𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄n≜infQXn+1|Xn𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄n​(QXn+1|Xn),≜subscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑛subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑛subscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛\mathsf{Risk}_{n}\triangleq\inf_{Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}}}\mathsf{Risk}_{n}(Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}}),(19)While (16) does not directly correspond to a statistical estimation problem, (19) is exactly the familiar setting of “density estimation”, where QXn+1|Xnsubscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}} is understood as an estimator for the distribution of the unseen Xn+1subscript𝑋𝑛1X_{n+1} based on the available data X1,…,Xnsubscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}.

In the Bayesian setting where θ𝜃\theta is drawn from a prior Pθsubscript𝑃𝜃P_{\theta}, the Bayes prediction risk coincides with the conditional mutual informationas a consequence of the variational representation (14):infQXn+1|Xn𝔼θ​[D​(PXn+1|Xn,θ​‖QXn+1|Xn|​PXn|θ)]=I​(θ;Xn+1|Xn).subscriptinfimumsubscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝔼𝜃delimited-[]𝐷subscript𝑃conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛𝜃delimited-‖|subscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃𝐼𝜃conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛\inf_{Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}}}\mathbb{E}_{\theta}[D(P_{X_{n+1}|X^{n},\theta}\|Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}}|P_{X^{n}|\theta})]=I(\theta;X_{n+1}|X^{n}).(20)Furthermore, the Bayes estimator that achieves this infimum takes the following form:QXn+1|Xn𝖡𝖺𝗒𝖾𝗌=PXn+1|Xn=∫ΘPXn+1|θ​𝑑Pθ∫ΘPXn|θ​𝑑Pθ,superscriptsubscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛𝖡𝖺𝗒𝖾𝗌subscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛subscriptΘsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛1𝜃differential-dsubscript𝑃𝜃subscriptΘsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃differential-dsubscript𝑃𝜃Q_{X_{n+1}|X^{n}}^{\sf Bayes}=P_{X^{n+1}|X^{n}}=\frac{\int_{\Theta}P_{X^{n+1}|\theta}\,dP_{\theta}}{\int_{\Theta}P_{X^{n}|\theta}\,dP_{\theta}},(21)known as the Bayes predictive density [Dav73, LB04].These representations play a crucial role in the lower bound proof of Theorem 1.Under appropriate conditions which hold for Markov models (see Lemma 33 in Appendix A), the minimax prediction risk (19) also admits a dual representation analogous to (17):𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄n=supθ∼πI​(θ;Xn+1|Xn),subscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑛subscriptsupremumsimilar-to𝜃𝜋𝐼𝜃conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑛1superscript𝑋𝑛\mathsf{Risk}_{n}=\sup_{\theta\sim\pi}I(\theta;X_{n+1}|X^{n}),(22)which, in view of (20), show that the principle of “minimax=worst-case Bayes” continues tohold for prediction problem in Markov models.

The following resultrelates the redundancy and the prediction risk.

The upper bound on the redundancy follows from the chain rule of KL divergence:D​(PXn|θ∥QXn)=∑t=1nD​(PXt|Xt−1,θ​‖QXt|Xt−1|​PXt−1).𝐷conditionalsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑛𝐷subscript𝑃conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑡superscript𝑋𝑡1𝜃delimited-‖|subscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑡superscript𝑋𝑡1subscript𝑃superscript𝑋𝑡1D(P_{X^{n}|\theta}\|Q_{X^{n}})=\sum_{t=1}^{n}D(P_{X_{t}|X^{t-1},\theta}\|Q_{X_{t}|X^{t-1}}|P_{X^{t-1}}).(27)Thussupθ∈ΘD​(PXn|θ∥QXn)≤∑t=1nsupθ∈ΘD​(PXt|Xt−1,θ​‖QXt|Xt−1|​PXt−1).subscriptsupremum𝜃Θ𝐷conditionalsubscript𝑃conditionalsuperscript𝑋𝑛𝜃subscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛superscriptsubscript𝑡1𝑛subscriptsupremum𝜃Θ𝐷subscript𝑃conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑡superscript𝑋𝑡1𝜃delimited-‖|subscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑡superscript𝑋𝑡1subscript𝑃superscript𝑋𝑡1\sup_{\theta\in\Theta}D(P_{X^{n}|\theta}\|Q_{X^{n}})\leq\sum_{t=1}^{n}\sup_{\theta\in\Theta}D(P_{X_{t}|X^{t-1},\theta}\|Q_{X_{t}|X^{t-1}}|P_{X^{t-1}}).Minimizing both sides over QXnsubscript𝑄superscript𝑋𝑛Q_{X^{n}} (or equivalently, QXt|Xt−1subscript𝑄conditionalsubscript𝑋𝑡superscript𝑋𝑡1Q_{X_{t}|X^{t-1}} for t=1,…,n𝑡1…𝑛t=1,\ldots,n) yields (23).

It remains to justify (33), which has a simple information-theoretic proof:Let T𝑇T denote the collection of sequences xnsuperscript𝑥𝑛x^{n} in [k]nsuperscriptdelimited-[]𝑘𝑛[k]^{n} whose type is given by (n1,…,nk)subscript𝑛1…subscript𝑛𝑘(n_{1},\ldots,n_{k}). In this section, we prove the lower bound part of Theorem 1, which shows the optimality of the average version of the add-one estimator (25).We first describe the lower bound construction for three-state chains, which is subsequently extended to k𝑘k states.

To show Theorem 8, consider the following one-parameter family of transition matrices:ℳ={Mp=[1−2n1n1n1n1−1n−pp1np1−1n−p]:0≤p≤1−1n}.ℳconditional-setsubscript𝑀𝑝delimited-[]matrix12𝑛1𝑛1𝑛1𝑛11𝑛𝑝𝑝1𝑛𝑝11𝑛𝑝0𝑝11𝑛{\mathcal{M}}=\left\{M_{p}=\left[\begin{matrix}1-\frac{2}{n}&\frac{1}{n}&\frac{1}{n}\\\frac{1}{n}&1-\frac{1}{n}-p&p\\\frac{1}{n}&p&1-\frac{1}{n}-p\end{matrix}\right]\colon 0\leq p\leq 1-\frac{1}{n}\right\}.(34)Note that each transition matrix in ℳℳ{\mathcal{M}} is symmetric (hence doubly stochastic), whose corresponding chain is reversible with a uniform stationary distribution and spectral gap Θ​(1n)Θ1𝑛\Theta(\frac{1}{n}); see Fig. 1.

The main idea is as follows.Notice that by design, with constant probability, the trajectory is of the following form: The chain starts and stays at state 1 for t𝑡t steps, and then transitions into state 2 or 3 and never returns to state 1, where t=1,…,n−1𝑡1…𝑛1t=1,\ldots,n-1. Since p𝑝p is the single unknown parameter, the only useful observations are visits to state 222 and 333 and each visit entails one observation about p𝑝p by flipping a coin with bias roughly p𝑝p. Thus the effective sample size for estimating p𝑝p is n−t−1𝑛𝑡1n-t-1 and we expect the best estimation error is of the order of 1n−t1𝑛𝑡\frac{1}{n-t}. However, t𝑡t is not fixed. In fact, conditioned on the trajectory is of this form, t𝑡t is roughly uniformly distributed between 111 and n−1𝑛1n-1. As such, we anticipate the estimation error of p𝑝p is approximately1n−1​∑i=1n−11n−t=Θ​(log⁡nn).1𝑛1superscriptsubscript𝑖1𝑛11𝑛𝑡Θ𝑛𝑛\frac{1}{n-1}\sum_{i=1}^{n-1}\frac{1}{n-t}=\Theta\left(\frac{\log n}{n}\right).Intuitively speaking, the construction in Fig. 1 “embeds” a symmetric two-state chain (with states 2 and 3) with unknown parameter p𝑝p into a space of three states, by adding a “nuisance” state 1, which effectively slows down the exploration of the useful part of the state space, so that in a trajectory of length n𝑛n, the effective number of observations we get to make about p𝑝p is roughly uniformly distributed between 111 and n𝑛n. This explains the extra log factor in Theorem 8, which actually stems from the harmonic sum in 𝔼​[1Uniform​([n])]𝔼delimited-[]1Uniformdelimited-[]𝑛\mathbb{E}[\frac{1}{\mathrm{Uniform}([n])}].We will fully explore this embedding idea in Section 3.2 to deal with larger state space.

Next we make the above intuition rigorous using a Bayesian argument.Let us start by recalling the following well-known lemma.

The lower bound construction for 333-state chains in Section 3.1can be generalized to k𝑘k-state chains.The high-level argument is again to augment a (k−1)𝑘1(k-1)-state chain into a k𝑘k-state chain. Specifically, we partition the state space [k]delimited-[]𝑘[k] into two sets 𝒮1={1}subscript𝒮11{\mathcal{S}}_{1}=\{1\} and 𝒮2={2,3,⋯,k}subscript𝒮223⋯𝑘{\mathcal{S}}_{2}=\{2,3,\cdots,k\}.Consider a k𝑘k-state Markov chain such that the transition probabilities from 𝒮1subscript𝒮1{\mathcal{S}}_{1} to 𝒮2subscript𝒮2{\mathcal{S}}_{2}, and from 𝒮2subscript𝒮2{\mathcal{S}}_{2} to 𝒮1subscript𝒮1{\mathcal{S}}_{1}, are both very small (on the order of Θ​(1/n)Θ1𝑛\Theta(1/n)). At state 111, the chain either stays at 111 with probability 1−1/n11𝑛1-1/n or moves to one of the states in 𝒮2subscript𝒮2{\mathcal{S}}_{2} with equal probability 1n​(k−1)1𝑛𝑘1\frac{1}{n(k-1)};at each state in 𝒮2subscript𝒮2{\mathcal{S}}_{2}, the chain moves to 111 with probability 1n1𝑛\frac{1}{n}; otherwise, within the state subspace 𝒮2subscript𝒮2{\mathcal{S}}_{2}, the chain evolves according to some symmetric transition matrix T𝑇T. (See Fig. 2 in Section 3.2.1 for the precise transition diagram.)

The key feature of such a chain is as follows. Let 𝒳tsubscript𝒳𝑡{\mathcal{X}}_{t} be the event that X1,X2,⋯,Xt∈𝒮1subscript𝑋1subscript𝑋2⋯subscript𝑋𝑡subscript𝒮1X_{1},X_{2},\cdots,X_{t}\in{\mathcal{S}}_{1} and Xt+1,⋯,Xn∈𝒮2subscript𝑋𝑡1⋯subscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝒮2X_{t+1},\cdots,X_{n}\in{\mathcal{S}}_{2}. For each t∈[n−1]𝑡delimited-[]𝑛1t\in[n-1],one can show that ℙ​(𝒳t)≥c/nℙsubscript𝒳𝑡𝑐𝑛\mathbb{P}({\mathcal{X}}_{t})\geq c/n for some absolute constant c>0𝑐0c>0. Moreover, conditioned on the event 𝒳tsubscript𝒳𝑡{\mathcal{X}}_{t}, (Xt+1,…,Xn)subscript𝑋𝑡1…subscript𝑋𝑛(X_{t+1},\ldots,X_{n}) is equal in law to a stationary Markov chain (Y1,⋯,Yn−t)subscript𝑌1⋯subscript𝑌𝑛𝑡(Y_{1},\cdots,Y_{n-t}) on state space 𝒮2subscript𝒮2{\mathcal{S}}_{2} with symmetric transition matrix T𝑇T.It is not hard to show that estimating M𝑀M and T𝑇T are nearly equivalent. Consider the Bayesian setting where T𝑇T is drawn from some prior. Next we rigorously carry out the lower bound proof sketched above: In Section 3.2.1, we explicitly construct the k𝑘k-state chain which satisfies the desired properties in Section 3.2. In Section 3.2.2, we make the steps in (3.2) precise and bound the Bayes risk from below by an appropriate mutual information. In Section 3.2.3, we choose a prior distribution on the transition probabilities and prove a lower bound on the resulting mutual information, thereby completing the proof of Theorem 1, with the added bonus that the construction is restricted to irreducible and reversible chains.

We construct a k𝑘k-state chain with the following transition probability matrix:M=[1−1n1n​(k−1)1n​(k−1)⋯1n​(k−1)1/n1/n⋮1/n(1−1n)​T],𝑀delimited-[]matrix11𝑛matrix1𝑛𝑘11𝑛𝑘1⋯1𝑛𝑘1matrix1𝑛1𝑛⋮1𝑛11𝑛𝑇\displaystyle M=\left[\begin{matrix}1-\frac{1}{n}&\begin{matrix}\frac{1}{n(k-1)}&\frac{1}{n(k-1)}&\cdots&\frac{1}{n(k-1)}\end{matrix}\\\begin{matrix}1/n\\1/n\\\vdots\\1/n\end{matrix}&\mbox{\LARGE$\left(1-\frac{1}{n}\right)T$}\end{matrix}\right],(44)where T∈ℝ𝒮2×𝒮2𝑇superscriptℝsubscript𝒮2subscript𝒮2T\in\mathbb{R}^{{\mathcal{S}}_{2}\times{\mathcal{S}}_{2}} is a symmetric stochastic matrix to be chosen later. The transition diagram of M𝑀M is shown in Figure 2.One can also verify that the spectral gap of M𝑀M is Θ​(1n)Θ1𝑛\Theta(\frac{1}{n}).

Let (X1,…,Xn)subscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛(X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}) be the trajectory of a stationary Markov chain with transition matrix M𝑀M.We observe the following properties:(P1)This Markov chain is irreducible and reversible, with stationary distribution (12,12​(k−1),⋯,12​(k−1))1212𝑘1⋯12𝑘1(\frac{1}{2},\frac{1}{2(k-1)},\cdots,\frac{1}{2(k-1)});(P2)For t∈[n−1]𝑡delimited-[]𝑛1t\in[n-1], let 𝒳tsubscript𝒳𝑡{\mathcal{X}}_{t} denote the collections of trajectories xnsuperscript𝑥𝑛x^{n} such that x1,x2,⋯,xt∈𝒮1subscript𝑥1subscript𝑥2⋯subscript𝑥𝑡subscript𝒮1x_{1},x_{2},\cdots,x_{t}\in{\mathcal{S}}_{1} and xt+1,⋯,xn∈𝒮2subscript𝑥𝑡1⋯subscript𝑥𝑛subscript𝒮2x_{t+1},\cdots,x_{n}\in{\mathcal{S}}_{2}. Let ℳk−1𝗌𝗒𝗆superscriptsubscriptℳ𝑘1𝗌𝗒𝗆{\mathcal{M}}_{k-1}^{\mathsf{sym}} be the collection of all symmetric transition matrices on state space 𝒮2={2,…,k}subscript𝒮22…𝑘{\mathcal{S}}_{2}=\{2,\ldots,k\}.Consider a Bayesian setting where the transition matrix M𝑀M is constructed in (44) and the submatrix T𝑇T is drawn from an arbitrary prior on ℳk−1𝗌𝗒𝗆superscriptsubscriptℳ𝑘1𝗌𝗒𝗆{\mathcal{M}}_{k-1}^{\mathsf{sym}}.The following lemma lower bounds the Bayes prediction risk.

Lemma 10 is the formal statement of the inequality (3.2) presented in the proof sketch. Maximizing the lower bound over the prior on T𝑇T and in view of the mutual information representation (17), we obtain the following corollary.

We make use of the properties (P1)–(P3) in Section 3.2.1 to prove Lemma 10.

Recall that in the Bayesian setting, we first draw T𝑇T from some prior on ℳk−1𝗌𝗒𝗆superscriptsubscriptℳ𝑘1𝗌𝗒𝗆{\mathcal{M}}_{k-1}^{\mathsf{sym}}, then generate the stationary Markov chain Xn=(X1,…,Xn)superscript𝑋𝑛subscript𝑋1…subscript𝑋𝑛X^{n}=(X_{1},\ldots,X_{n}) with state space [k]delimited-[]𝑘[k] and transition matrix M𝑀M in (44), and (Y1,…,Yn)subscript𝑌1…subscript𝑌𝑛(Y_{1},\ldots,Y_{n}) with state space 𝒮2={2,…,k}subscript𝒮22…𝑘{\mathcal{S}}_{2}=\{2,\ldots,k\} and transition matrix T𝑇T.

In view of Lemma 10, it remains to find a prior on ℳk−1𝗌𝗒𝗆superscriptsubscriptℳ𝑘1𝗌𝗒𝗆{\mathcal{M}}_{k-1}^{\mathsf{sym}} for T𝑇T, such that the mutual information I​(T;Yn)𝐼𝑇superscript𝑌𝑛I(T;Y^{n}) is large.We make use of the connection identified in [DMPW81, Dav83, Ris84] between estimation error and mutual information (see also[CS04, Theorem 7.1] for a self-contained exposition).To lower the mutual information, a key step is to find a good estimator T^​(Yn)^𝑇superscript𝑌𝑛\widehat{T}(Y^{n}) of T𝑇T. This is carried out in the following lemma.

Since T𝑇T is symmetric, the stationary distribution is uniform, and there is a one-to-one correspondence between the joint distribution of (Y1,Y2)subscript𝑌1subscript𝑌2(Y_{1},Y_{2}) and the transition probabilities. Notice that the prediction problem consists of k𝑘k sub-problems of estimating the individual rows of M𝑀M, so it suffices show the contribution from each of them is O​(kn)𝑂𝑘𝑛O\left(\frac{k}{n}\right). In particular, assuming the chain terminates in state 1 we bound the risk of estimating the first row by the add-one estimator M^+1​(j|1)=N1​j+1​\over​N1+ksuperscript^𝑀1conditional𝑗1subscript𝑁1𝑗1\oversubscript𝑁1𝑘\widehat{M}^{+1}(j|1)={N_{1j}+1\over N_{1}+k}.Under the absolute spectral gap condition of γ∗≥γ0subscript𝛾subscript𝛾0\gamma_{*}\geq\gamma_{0}, we show𝔼[𝟏{Xn=1}D(M(⋅|1)∥M^+1(⋅|1))]≲k\overn(1+log⁡k​\over​k​γ04).\displaystyle\mathbb{E}\left[{\mathbf{1}_{\left\{{X_{n}=1}\right\}}}D\left({M(\cdot|1)}\|{\widehat{M}^{+1}(\cdot|1)}\right)\right]\lesssim{k\over n}\left(1+\sqrt{\log k\over k\gamma_{0}^{4}}\right).(67)By symmetry,we get the desired 𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄k,n​(γ0)≲k2​\over​n​(1+log⁡k​\over​k​γ04)less-than-or-similar-tosubscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑘𝑛subscript𝛾0superscript𝑘2\over𝑛1𝑘\over𝑘superscriptsubscript𝛾04\mathsf{Risk}_{k,n}(\gamma_{0})\lesssim{k^{2}\over n}\left(1+\sqrt{\log k\over k\gamma_{0}^{4}}\right).The basic steps of our analysis are as follows:•When N1subscript𝑁1N_{1} is substantially smaller than its mean, we can bound the risk using the worst-case risk bound for add-one estimators and theprobability of this rare event.•Otherwise, we decompose the prediction risk asD(M(⋅|1)∥M^+1(⋅|1))=∑j=1k[M(j|1)log(M(j|1)(N1+k)\overN1​j+1)−M(j|1)+N1​j+1\overN1+k].\displaystyle D(M(\cdot|1)\|\widehat{M}^{+1}(\cdot|1))=\sum_{j=1}^{k}\left[M(j|1)\log\left(M(j|1)(N_{1}+k)\over N_{1j}+1\right)-M(j|1)+{N_{1j}+1\over N_{1}+k}\right].We then analyze each term depending on whether N1​jsubscript𝑁1𝑗N_{1j} is typical or not.Unless N1​jsubscript𝑁1𝑗N_{1j} is atypically small, the add-one estimator works well whose risk can be bounded quadratically.

To analyze the concentration of the empirical counts we use the following moment bounds. The proofs are deferred to Appendix B.

When γ∗subscript𝛾\gamma_{*} is high this shows that the moments behave as if for each i∈[k]𝑖delimited-[]𝑘i\in[k], N1subscript𝑁1N_{1} is approximately Binomial(n−1,πi𝑛1subscript𝜋𝑖n-1,\pi_{i}) and Ni​jsubscript𝑁𝑖𝑗N_{ij} is approximately Binomial(Ni,M​(j|i))subscript𝑁𝑖𝑀conditional𝑗𝑖(N_{i},M(j|i)), which happens in case of iid sampling. For iid models [KOPS15] showed that the add-one estimator achieves O​(kn)𝑂𝑘𝑛O\left(\frac{k}{n}\right) risk bound which we aim here too. In addition, dependency of the above moments on γ∗subscript𝛾\gamma_{*} gives rise to sufficient conditions that guarantees parametric rate. The technical details are given below.

We show the following monotonicity result of the prediction risk.In view of this result, Corollary 4 immediately follows from Theorem 2 and Theorem 3 (i).Intuitively, the optimal prediction risk is monotonically increasing with the number of states; this, however, does not follow immediately due to the extra assumptions of irreducibility, reversibility, and prescribed spectral gap.

In view of the proof of (93), we note that any distribution π𝜋\pi on 𝒮msuperscript𝒮𝑚{\mathcal{S}}^{m} and mthsuperscript𝑚thm^{\text{th}}-order transition matrix M𝑀M satisfying π​(xm)=π​(xm¯)𝜋superscript𝑥𝑚𝜋¯superscript𝑥𝑚\pi(x^{m})=\pi(\overline{x^{m}}) and (94) also satisfy (91). This implies such a π𝜋\pi is a stationary distribution for M𝑀M. In view of Lemma 20 the above conditions also guarantee reversibility. This observation can be summarized in the following lemma, which will be used to prove the reversibility of specific Markov chains later.

For mthsuperscript𝑚thm^{\rm th}-order stationary Markov chains, the optimal prediction risk is defined as as𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄k,n,msubscript𝖱𝗂𝗌𝗄𝑘𝑛𝑚\displaystyle{\mathsf{Risk}}_{k,n,m}≜infM^supM𝔼[D(M(⋅|Xn−m+1n)∥M^(⋅|Xn−m+1n))]\displaystyle\triangleq\inf_{\widehat{M}}\sup_{M}\mathbb{E}[D(M(\cdot|X_{n-m+1}^{n})\|\widehat{M}(\cdot|X_{n-m+1}^{n}))]=infM^supM∑xm∈𝒮m𝔼[D(M(⋅|xm)∥M^(⋅|xm))𝟏{Xn−m+1n=xm}]\displaystyle=\inf_{\widehat{M}}\sup_{M}\sum_{x^{m}\in{\mathcal{S}}^{m}}\mathbb{E}[D(M(\cdot|x^{m})\|\widehat{M}(\cdot|x^{m})){\mathbf{1}_{\left\{{X_{n-m+1}^{n}=x^{m}}\right\}}}](96)where the supremum is taken over all km×ksuperscript𝑘𝑚𝑘k^{m}\times k stochastic matrices M𝑀M and the trajectory is initiated from the stationary distribution.In the remainder of this section we will show the following result, completing the proof of Theorem 5 previously announced in Section 1.

We prove the upper bound part of the preceding theorem, using only stationarity (not reversibility).We rely on techniques from [CS04, Chapter 6, Page 486] for proving redundancy bounds for the mthsuperscript𝑚thm^{\text{th}}-order chains. We only analyze the case m=2𝑚2m=2, i.e. second-order Markov chains with binary states, as the lower bound still applies to the case of m≥3𝑚3m\geq 3 case. The transition matrix for second-order chains is given by a k2×ksuperscript𝑘2𝑘k^{2}\times k stochastic matrices M𝑀M that gives the transition probability from the ordered pairs (i,j)∈𝒮×𝒮𝑖𝑗𝒮𝒮(i,j)\in{\mathcal{S}}\times{\mathcal{S}} to some state ℓ∈𝒮ℓ𝒮\ell\in{\mathcal{S}}:M(ℓ|ij)=ℙ[X3=ℓ|X1=i,X2=j].\displaystyle M(\ell|ij)=\mathbb{P}\left[X_{3}=\ell|X_{1}=i,X_{2}=j\right].(101)Our result is the following.

The upper bound part has been shown in Lemma 23.For the lower bound, consider the following one-parametric family of transition matrices (we replace 𝒮𝒮{\mathcal{S}} by {1,2}12\left\{1,2\right\} for simplicity of the notation)ℳ~={Mp=​[ 12111−1n1n211n1−1n121−pp22p1−p]:0≤p≤1}~ℳconditional-setsubscript𝑀𝑝delimited-[]fragments 12fragments11fragments11𝑛1𝑛fragments211𝑛fragments11𝑛fragments12fragments1p𝑝fragments22𝑝fragments1p0𝑝1\displaystyle\widetilde{\mathcal{M}}=\left\{M_{p}=\hbox{}\;\vbox{\kern 40.12498pt\hbox{$\kern 60.61786pt\kern-4.75pt\left[\kern-60.61786pt\vbox{\kern-40.12498pt\vbox{\halign{$#$\hfil\kern 2\p@\kern\@tempdima&\thinspace\hfil$#$\hfil&&\quad\hfil$#$\hfil\cr\hfil\crcr\kern-12.0pt\cr~{}$\hfil\kern 2.0pt\kern 4.75pt&1&2\crcr\kern 2.0pt\cr 11$\hfil\kern 2.0pt\kern 4.75pt&1-\frac{1}{n}&\frac{1}{n}\cr 21$\hfil\kern 2.0pt\kern 4.75pt&\frac{1}{n}&1-\frac{1}{n}\cr 12$\hfil\kern 2.0pt\kern 4.75pt&1-p&p\cr 22$\hfil\kern 2.0pt\kern 4.75pt&p&1-p\crcr\cr}}\kern-12.0pt}\,\right]$}}:0\leq p\leq 1\right\}(109)and place a uniform prior on p∈[0,1]𝑝01p\in[0,1]. Next we introduce the set of trajectories based on which we will lower bound the prediction risk. We will prove the following.

The following lemma bounds the pseudo spectral gap from below.

∎

We discuss the assumptions and implications of our results as well as related open problems.

Theorem 1 determines the optimal prediction risk under the assumption of k≲nless-than-or-similar-to𝑘𝑛k\lesssim\sqrt{n}.When k≳ngreater-than-or-equivalent-to𝑘𝑛k\gtrsim\sqrt{n}, Theorem 1 shows that the KL risk is bounded away from zero. However, as the KL risk can be as large as log⁡k𝑘\log k, it is a meaningful question to determine the optimal rate in this case, which, thanks to the general reduction in (11), reduces to determining the redundancy for symmetric and general Markov chains.For iid data, the minimax pointwise redundancy is known to be n​log⁡kn+O​(n2k)𝑛𝑘𝑛𝑂superscript𝑛2𝑘n\log\frac{k}{n}+O(\frac{n^{2}}{k}) [SW12, Theorem 1] when k≫nmuch-greater-than𝑘𝑛k\gg n.Since the average and pointwise redundancy usually behave similarly, for Markov chains it is reasonable to conjecture that the redundancy is Θ​(n​log⁡k2n)Θ𝑛superscript𝑘2𝑛\Theta(n\log\frac{k^{2}}{n}) in the large alphabet regime of k≳ngreater-than-or-equivalent-to𝑘𝑛k\gtrsim\sqrt{n}, which, in view of (11), would imply the optimal prediction risk isΘ​(log⁡k2n)Θsuperscript𝑘2𝑛\Theta(\log\frac{k^{2}}{n}) for k≫nmuch-greater-than𝑘𝑛k\gg\sqrt{n}.In comparison, we note that the prediction risk is at most log⁡k𝑘\log k, achieved by the uniform distribution.

As mentioned in Section 1.1, standard arguments based on concentration inequalities inevitably rely on mixing conditions such as the spectral gap. In contrast, the risk bound in Theorem 1, which is free of any mixing condition, is enabled by powerful techniques from universal compression which bound the redundancy by the pointwise maximum over all trajectories combined with information-theoretic or combinatorial argument. This program only relies on the Markovity of the process rather than stationarity or spectral gap assumptions.The limitation of this approach, however, is that the reduction between prediction and redundancy crucially depends on the form of the KL loss function444In fact, this connection breaks down if one swap M𝑀M and M^^𝑀\widehat{M} in the KL divergence in (1). in (1), which allows one to use the mutual information representation and the chain rule to relate individual risks to the cumulative risk.More general loss in terms of f𝑓f-divergence have been considered in [HOP18]. Obtaining spectral gap-independent risk bound for these loss functions, this time without the aid of universal compression, is an open question.

As mentioned above, the redundancy result in Lemma 7 (see also [Dav83, TJW18]) holds for nonstationary Markov chains as well.However, our redundancy-based risk upper bound in Lemma 6 crucially relies on stationarity.It is unclear whether the result of Theorem 1 carries over to nonstationary chains.
